<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png/220px-Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png/330px-Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png/440px-Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilayat_al_Sudan_al_Gharbi_maximum_territorial_control.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The maximum extent of Boko Haram, in January 2015, shown in dark grey</div>

<p><b>Boko Haram</b>, referred to by themselves as <b>al-Wilāyat al-Islāmiyya Gharb Afrīqiyyah</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">الولاية الإسلامية غرب أفريقيا</span>‎‎, (Islamic State West Africa Province, <i>ISWAP</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-name-15">[15]</a></sup> and <b>Jamā'at Ahl as-Sunnah lid-Da'wah wa'l-Jihād</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">جماعة أهل السنة للدعوة والجهاد</span>‎‎, "Group of the People of Sunnah for Preaching and Jihad"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> is an Islamic extremist group based in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, also active in <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bureau_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bureau-10">[10]</a></sup> The group was led by <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Shekau" title="Abubakar Shekau">Abubakar Shekau</a> until August 2016, when he was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musab_al-Barnawi" title="Abu Musab al-Barnawi">Abu Musab al-Barnawi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnawi_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnawi-1">[1]</a></sup> The group had alleged links to <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a>, but in March 2015, it announced its allegiance to the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BokoHaramIS_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-BokoHaramIS-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BokoHaramIS2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BokoHaramIS2-18">[18]</a></sup> Since the <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram_insurgency" title="Boko Haram insurgency">current insurgency started in 2009</a>, it has killed 20,000 and displaced 2.3 million from their homes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-18-11-15_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-18-11-15-19">[19]</a></sup> and was ranked as the world's deadliest terror group by the <a href="/wiki/Global_Terrorism_Index" title="Global Terrorism Index">Global Terrorism Index</a> in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITI_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITI-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEP_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>After its founding in 2002, Boko Haram's increasing radicalization led to a violent uprising in July 2009 in which its leader was <a href="/wiki/Summary_execution" title="Summary execution">summarily executed</a>. Its unexpected resurgence, following a mass prison break in September 2010, was accompanied by increasingly sophisticated attacks, initially against <a href="/wiki/Soft_target" title="Soft target">soft targets</a>, and progressing in 2011 to include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suicide_bombing" title="Suicide bombing">suicide bombings</a> of police buildings and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> office in <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a>. The government's establishment of a <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> at the beginning of 2012, extended in the following year to cover the entire northeast of Nigeria, led to an increase in both security force abuses and militant attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hundreds_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-hundreds-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the 2.3 million people displaced by the conflict since May 2013, at least 250,000 have left Nigeria and fled into <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> or <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Boko Haram killed over 6,600 in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The group have carried out mass abductions including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chibok_schoolgirl_kidnapping" title="Chibok schoolgirl kidnapping">kidnapping of 276 schoolgirls</a> from <a href="/wiki/Chibok" title="Chibok">Chibok</a> in April 2014. Corruption in the security services and human rights abuses committed by them have hampered efforts to counter the unrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw_report_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrw_report-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-2014, the militants gained control of swathes of territory in and around their home state of <a href="/wiki/Borno_State" title="Borno State">Borno</a>, estimated at 50,000 square kilometres (20,000 sq mi) in January 2015, but did not capture the state capital, <a href="/wiki/Maiduguri" title="Maiduguri">Maiduguri</a>, where the group was originally based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In September 2015, the Director of Information at the Defence Headquarters of Nigeria announced that all Boko Haram camps had been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defeat_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-defeat-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The group's official name is <b>Wilayat Gharb Afriqiya</b>, to designate it as a branch or "province" of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-name-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iswMay15_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-iswMay15-33">[33]</a></sup> Prior to Abubakar Shekau's pledge of allegiance to ISIL, the group's official name was <b>Jamā'atu Ahli is-Sunnah lid-Da'wati wal-Jihād</b> <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">جماعة أهل السنة للدعوة والجهاد</span>, meaning "People Committed to the Prophet's Teachings for Propagation and Jihad".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name "Boko Haram" is usually translated as "Western education is forbidden". <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">Haram</a> is from the Arabic <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">حَرَام</span> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%B8%A4ar%C4%81m" title="Ḥarām">ḥarām</a></i>, "forbidden"; and the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a> word <i>boko</i> [the first vowel is long, the second pronounced in a low tone], meaning "fake",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-George_Percy_Bargery_1934_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-George_Percy_Bargery_1934-36">[36]</a></sup> which is used to refer to secular Western education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boko_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-boko-37">[37]</a></sup> Boko Haram has also been translated as "Western influence is a sin"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and "Westernization is sacrilege".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-enc-23">[23]</a></sup> Until the death of its founder <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Yusuf_(Boko_Haram)" title="Mohammed Yusuf (Boko Haram)">Mohammed Yusuf</a>, the group was also reportedly known as <i>Yusifiyya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wikileaks_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-wikileaks-39">[39]</a></sup> Northern Nigerians have commonly dismissed Western education as <i>ilimin boko</i> ("fake education") and secular schools as <i>makaranta boko</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-George_Percy_Bargery_1934_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-George_Percy_Bargery_1934-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boko_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-boko-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dr._Aliyu_U._Tilde_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dr._Aliyu_U._Tilde-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ideology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NG-Sharia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="400" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/NG-Sharia.png/220px-NG-Sharia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/NG-Sharia.png/330px-NG-Sharia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/NG-Sharia.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NG-Sharia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nigerian states with sharia law shown in green</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Boko Haram was founded as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> Islamic fundamentalist sect, influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi movement</a>, advocating a strict form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharia_law" title="Sharia law">Sharia law</a>. It developed into a <a href="/wiki/Salafi_jihadism" title="Salafi jihadism">Salafist-jihadi</a> group in 2009. The movement is diffuse, and fighters associated with it do not necessarily follow Salafi doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Congressional_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Congressional-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfrBackgrounder_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfrBackgrounder-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTC_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-CTC-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Onuoha_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Onuoha-46">[46]</a></sup> The group has denounced the members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiite" title="Shiite">Shiite</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Izala_Society" title="Izala Society">Izala</a> sects as infidels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Boko Haram seeks the establishment of an Islamic state in Nigeria. It opposes the Westernization of Nigerian society and the concentration of the wealth of the country among members of a small political elite, mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> south of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WJD_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-WJD-49">[49]</a></sup> Nigeria is Africa's biggest economy, but 60% of its population of 173 million (2013) live on less than $1 a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The sharia law imposed by local authorities, beginning with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zamfara" title="Zamfara">Zamfara</a> in January 2000 and covering 12 northern states by late 2002, may have promoted links between Boko Haram and political leaders, but was considered by the group to have been corrupted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journals_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-journals-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:101</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNHCR_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNHCR-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Borno <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">Sheik</a> Fatahi, Yusuf was trained by Kano <a href="/wiki/Salafi_movement" title="Salafi movement">Salafi</a> Izala Sheik <a href="/wiki/Ja%27afar_Mahmud_Adam" title="Ja'afar Mahmud Adam">Ja'afar Mahmud Adamu</a>, who called him the "leader of young people"; the two split some time in 2002–04. They both preached in <a href="/wiki/Maiduguri" title="Maiduguri">Maiduguri</a>'s Indimi Mosque, which was attended by the deputy governor of Borno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wikileaks_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-wikileaks-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Many of the group were reportedly inspired by Mohammed Marwa, known as <a href="/wiki/Maitatsine" title="Maitatsine">Maitatsine</a> ("He who curses others"), a self-proclaimed prophet (<i>annabi</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a> word usually used only to describe the founder of Islam) born in <a href="/wiki/British_Cameroons" title="British Cameroons">Northern Cameroon</a> who condemned the reading of books other than the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-George_Percy_Bargery_1934_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-George_Percy_Bargery_1934-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-managing_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-managing-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-west_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-west-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In a 2009 <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> interview, Yusuf, described by analysts as being well-educated, reaffirmed his opposition to Western education. He rejected the theory of evolution, said that rain is not "an evaporation caused by the sun", and that the Earth is <a href="/wiki/Flat_Earth" title="Flat Earth">not a sphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc20090728_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20090728-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Nigerian_Republic" title="Fourth Nigerian Republic">Fourth Nigerian Republic</a></div>
<p>Before <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a> and subsequent annexation into the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in 1900 as <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Nigeria" title="Colonial Nigeria">Colonial Nigeria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a> ruled the territory where Boko Haram is currently active. It was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereign" title="Sovereign">sovereign</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate" title="Sultanate">sultanate</a> run according to the principles of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Medina" title="Constitution of Medina">Constitution of Medina</a>, with a majority <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a> Muslim population. In 1903, both the <a href="/wiki/Borno_Emirate" title="Borno Emirate">Borno Emirate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a> came under the control of the British. Christian missionaries at this time spread the Christian message in the region and had many converts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria-62">[62]</a></sup> British occupation ended with Nigerian independence in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc20110826_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20110826-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Except for a brief period of civilian rule between 1979 and 1983, Nigeria was governed by a series of military dictatorships from 1966 until the advent of democracy in 1999. <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_violence" title="Ethnic violence">Ethnic militancy</a> is thought to have been one of the causes of the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">1967–70 civil war</a>; <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence_in_Nigeria" title="Religious violence in Nigeria">religious violence</a> reached a new height in 1980 in <a href="/wiki/Kano" title="Kano">Kano</a>, the largest city in the north of the country, where the Muslim fundamentalist sect <a href="/wiki/Yan_Tatsine" title="Yan Tatsine">Yan Tatsine</a> ("followers of Maitatsine") instigated riots that resulted in four or five thousand deaths. In the ensuing military crackdown, Maitatsine was killed, fuelling a backlash of increased violence that spread across other northern cities over the next twenty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Ewi_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Ewi-66">[66]</a></sup> Social inequality and poverty contributed both to the Maitatsine and Boko Haram uprisings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journals_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-journals-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:97–98</sup></p>
<p>In the decades since the end of British occupation, politicians and academics from the mainly Islamic North have expressed their fundamental opposition to Western education. Political ethno-religious interest groups, whose membership includes influential political, military and religious leaders, have thrived in Nigeria, though they were largely suppressed under military rule. Their <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> wings, formed since the country's return to civilian rule, have been implicated in much of the sectarian violence in the years following. The <a href="/wiki/Arewa_People%27s_Congress" title="Arewa People's Congress">Arewa People's Congress</a>, the militia wing of the <a href="/wiki/Arewa_Consultative_Forum" title="Arewa Consultative Forum">Arewa Consultative Forum</a>, the main political group representing the interests of northern Nigeria, is a well-funded group with military and intelligence expertise, and is considered capable of engaging in military action, including covert bombing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Founding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Yusuf_(Boko_Haram)" title="Mohammed Yusuf (Boko Haram)">Mohammed Yusuf</a> founded the sect that became known as Boko Haram in 2002 in Maiduguri, the capital of the north-eastern state of Borno. He established a religious complex and school that attracted poor Muslim families from across Nigeria and neighbouring countries. The center had the political goal of creating an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a>, and became a recruiting ground for <a href="/wiki/Jihadism" title="Jihadism">jihadis</a>. By denouncing the police and state corruption, Yusuf attracted followers from unemployed youths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfrBackgrounder_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-cfrBackgrounder-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc20110826_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20110826-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRIN_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRIN-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ1405_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-econ1405-69">[69]</a></sup> It has been speculated that the reason Yusuf founded Boko Haram appears to be that he saw an opportunity to exploit public outrage at government corruption by linking it to Western influence in governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GatestoneInstitute_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-GatestoneInstitute-70">[70]</a></sup> He is reported to have used the existing infrastructure in Borno of the <a href="/wiki/Izala_Society" title="Izala Society">Izala Society</a> (Jama'at Izalatil Bidiawa Iqamatus Sunnah), a popular conservative Islamic sect, to recruit members, before breaking away to form his own faction. The Izala were originally welcomed into government, along with people sympathetic to Yusuf. Boko Haram conducted its operations more or less peacefully during the first seven years of its existence, withdrawing from society into remote north-eastern areas. The government repeatedly ignored warnings about the increasingly militant character of the organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTC_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-CTC-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-accused_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-accused-71">[71]</a></sup> The Council of Ulama advised the government and the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Television_Authority" title="Nigerian Television Authority">Nigerian Television Authority</a> not to broadcast Yusuf's preaching, but their warnings were ignored. Yusuf's arrest elevated him to hero status. Borno's Deputy Governor Alhaji Dibal has reportedly claimed that <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> had ties with Boko Haram, but broke them when they decided that Yusuf was an unreliable person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wikileaks_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-wikileaks-39">[39]</a></sup> Stephen Davis, a former Anglican clergyman who has negotiated with Boko Haram many times blames local Nigerian politicians who support local bandits like Boko Haram in order for them to make life difficult for their political opponents. In particular Davis has blamed the former governor of Borno State <a href="/wiki/Ali_Modu_Sheriff" title="Ali Modu Sheriff">Ali Modu Sheriff</a> who initially supported Boko Haram but no longer needed them after the 2007 elections and stopped funding them but they were then out of control. Sheriff denies the accusations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamb_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lamb-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_of_violence">Campaign of violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Campaign of violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ni-map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="331" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Ni-map.png/300px-Ni-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/Ni-map.png 1.5x" width="300"/></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2009">2009</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2009">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2009_Boko_Haram_Uprising" title="2009 Boko Haram Uprising">2009 Boko Haram Uprising</a></div>
<p>In 2009, police began an investigation into the group code-named 'Operation Flush'. On July 26, security forces arrested nine Boko Haram members and confiscated weapons and bomb-making equipment. Either this or a clash with police during a funeral procession led to revenge attacks on police and widespread rioting. A joint military task force operation was launched in response, and by 30 July more than 700 people had been killed, mostly Boko Haram members, and police stations, prisons, government offices, schools and churches had been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-enc-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journals_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-journals-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:98–102</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigeria:_Boko_Haram_101_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nigeria:_Boko_Haram_101-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOSSITER_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOSSITER-74">[74]</a></sup> Yusuf was arrested, and died in custody "while trying to escape". As had been the case decades earlier in the wake of the 1980 Kano riots, the killing of the leader of an extremist group would have unintended consequences. He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Shekau" title="Abubakar Shekau">Abubakar Shekau</a>, formerly his second-in-command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> A classified <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_cable" title="Diplomatic cable">cable</a> apparently sent from the U.S. Embassy in <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a> in November 2009, has been published on <a href="/wiki/WikiLeaks" title="WikiLeaks">WikiLeaks</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wikileaks_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-wikileaks-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[Borno political and religious leaders] ... asserted that the state and federal government responded appropriately and, apart from the opposition party, overwhelmingly supported Yusuf's death without misgivings over the extrajudicial killing. Security remained a concern in Borno, with residents expressing concern about importation of arms and exchanges of religious messages across porous international borders.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to the leaked document, there were reports that Yusuf's deputy had survived, and audio tapes were believed to be in circulation in which Boko Haram threatened future attacks. Nevertheless, many observers did not anticipate imminent bloodshed. Security in Borno was downgraded. Borno government official Alhaji Boguma believed that the state deserved praise from the international community for ending the conflict in such a short time, and that the "wave of fundamentalism" had been "crushed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wikileaks_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-wikileaks-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010">2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bauchi_prison_break" title="Bauchi prison break">Bauchi prison break</a></div>
<p>In September 2010, having regrouped under their new leader, Boko Haram broke 105 of its members out of prison in <a href="/wiki/Bauchi" title="Bauchi">Bauchi</a> along with over 600 other prisoners and went on to launch attacks in several areas of northern Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Ewi_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Ewi-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boko_Haram_attacks_.E2.80.93_timeline_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boko_Haram_attacks_.E2.80.93_timeline-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peace_and_Security_Council_Report_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peace_and_Security_Council_Report-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011">2011</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2011">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under Shekau's leadership, the group continuously improved its operational capabilities. After launching a string of <a href="/wiki/Improvised_explosive_device" title="Improvised explosive device">IED</a> attacks against soft targets, and its first vehicle-borne IED attack in June 2011, killing 6 at the <a href="/wiki/Abuja" title="Abuja">Abuja</a> police headquarters, in August Boko Haram bombed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> headquarters in Abuja, the first time they had struck a Western target. A spokesman claiming responsibility for the attack, in which 11 UN staff members died as well as 12 others, with more than 100 injured, warned of future planned attacks on US and Nigerian government interests. Speaking soon after the US embassy's announcement of the arrival in the country of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a>, he went on to announce Boko Haram's terms for negotiation: the release of all imprisoned members. The increased sophistication of the group led observers to speculate that Boko Haram was affiliated with <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Islamic_Maghreb" title="Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb">Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb</a> (AQIM), which was active in <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boko_Haram_attacks_.E2.80.93_timeline_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boko_Haram_attacks_.E2.80.93_timeline-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peace_and_Security_Council_Report_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peace_and_Security_Council_Report-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Counterterrorism_2014_Calendar_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Counterterrorism_2014_Calendar-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boko Haram has maintained a steady rate of attacks since 2011, striking a wide range of targets, multiple times per week. They have attacked politicians, religious leaders, security forces and civilian targets. The tactic of <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide bombing</a>, used in the two attacks in the capital on the police and UN headquarters, was new to Nigeria. In Africa as a whole, it had only been used by <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)" title="Al-Shabaab (militant group)">al-Shabaab</a> in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> and, to a lesser extent, AQIM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bureau_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bureau-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-enc-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Counterterrorism_2014_Calendar_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Counterterrorism_2014_Calendar-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Dowden_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Dowden-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_inauguration">Presidential inauguration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Presidential inauguration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_presidential_election,_2011" title="Nigerian presidential election, 2011">Nigerian presidential election, 2011</a></div>
<p>Within hours of <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a>'s presidential inauguration in May 2011, Boko Haram carried out a series of bombings in <a href="/wiki/Bauchi" title="Bauchi">Bauchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaria" title="Zaria">Zaria</a> and Abuja. The most successful of these was the attack on the army barracks in Bauchi. A spokesman for the group told BBC Hausa that the attack had been carried out, as a test of loyalty, by serving members of the military hoping to join the group. This charge was later refuted by an army spokesman, who claimed, "This is not a <a href="/wiki/Banana_republic" title="Banana republic">banana republic</a>." However, on 8 January 2012, the president would announce that Boko Haram had in reality infiltrated both the army and the police, as well as the executive, parliamentary and legislative branches of government. Boko Haram's spokesman also claimed responsibility for the killing outside his home in Maiduguri of the politician Abba Anas Ibn Umar Garbai, the younger brother of the Shehu of Borno, who was the second most prominent Muslim in the country after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Sokoto" title="Sultan of Sokoto">Sultan of Sokoto</a>. He added, "We are doing what we are doing to fight injustice, if they stop their satanic ways of doing things and the injustices, we would stop what we are doing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>This was one of several political and religious assassinations Boko Haram carried out that year, with the presumed intention of correcting injustices in the group's home state of Borno. Meanwhile, the trail of massacres continued relentlessly, apparently leading the country towards civil war. By the end of 2011, these conflicting strategies led observers to question the group's cohesion; comparisons were drawn with the diverse motivations of the militant factions of the oil-rich <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta" title="Niger Delta">Niger Delta</a>. Adding to the confusion, in November, the <a href="/wiki/State_Security_Service_(Nigeria)" title="State Security Service (Nigeria)">State Security Service</a> announced that four criminal syndicates were operating under the name 'Boko Haram'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Dowden_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Dowden-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JEAN_HERSKOVITS_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-JEAN_HERSKOVITS-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>The common theme throughout the north-east was the targeting of police, who were regularly massacred at work or in <a href="/wiki/Drive-by_shooting" title="Drive-by shooting">drive-by shootings</a> at their homes, either in revenge for the killing of Yusuf, or as representatives of the state apparatus, or for no particular reason. Five officers were arrested for Yusuf's murder, which had no noticeable effect on the level of unrest. Opportunities for criminal enterprise flourished. Hundreds of police were dead and more than 60 police stations had been attacked by mid-2012. The government's response to this self-reinforcing trend towards insecurity was to invest heavily in security equipment, spending $5.5 billion, 20 percent of their overall budget, on bomb detection units, communications and transport; and $470 million on a <a href="/wiki/Africa%E2%80%93China_relations" title="Africa–China relations">Chinese</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCTV" title="CCTV">CCTV</a> system for Abuja, which has failed in its purpose of detecting or deterring acts of <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terror</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JEAN_HERSKOVITS_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-JEAN_HERSKOVITS-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HRW2012_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-HRW2012-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The election defeat of former military dictator <a href="/wiki/Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Muhammadu Buhari">Muhammadu Buhari</a> increased ethno-religious political tensions, as it broke the terms of a tacit agreement that the presidency would alternate after two terms of office between candidates from the Christian south and Muslim north of the country. Sectarian riots engulfed the twelve northern states of the country during the three days following the election, leaving more than 800 dead and 65,000 displaced. The subsequent campaign of violence by Boko Haram culminated in a string of bombings across the country on Christmas Day. In the outskirts of Abuja, 37 died in a church that had its roof blown off. One resident commented, "Cars were in flames and bodies littered everywhere", a phrase commonly repeated in international press reports about the bombings. Similar Christmas events had been reported in previous years. Jonathan declared a <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> on New Year's Eve in local government areas of Jos, Borno, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yobe" title="Yobe">Yobe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niger_State" title="Niger State">Niger</a>, and closed the international border in the north-east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FalolaHeaton2008_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FalolaHeaton2008-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012">2012</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2012">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="State_of_emergency">State of emergency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: State of emergency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Boko Haram carried out 115 attacks in 2011, killing 550. The state of emergency would usher in an intensification of violence. The opening three weeks of 2012 accounted for more than half of the death total of the preceding year. Two days after the state of emergency was declared, Boko Haram released an ultimatum to southern Nigerians living in the north, giving them three days to leave. Three days later they began a series of mostly small-scale attacks on Christians and members of the <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> ethnic group, causing hundreds to flee. In Kano, on 20 January, they carried out by far their most deadly action yet, an assault on police buildings, killing 190. One of the victims was a TV reporter. The attacks included a combined use of car bombs, suicide bombers and IEDs, supported by uniformed gunmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Congressional_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Congressional-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIKE_OBOH_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIKE_OBOH-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a> published reports in 2012 that were widely quoted by government agencies and the media, based on research conducted over the course of the conflict in the worst affected areas of the country. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGO" title="NGO">NGOs</a> were critical of both security forces and Boko Haram. HRW stated "Boko Haram should immediately cease all attacks, and threats of attacks, that cause loss of life, injury, and destruction of property. The Nigerian government should take urgent measures to address the human rights abuses that have helped fuel the violent militancy." According to the 2012 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Department_of_State" title="US Department of State">US Department of State</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States%27_Country_Reports_on_Human_Rights_Practices" title="United States' Country Reports on Human Rights Practices">Country Report on Human Rights Practices</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrw_report_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-hrw_report-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"... serious <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Nigeria" title="Human rights in Nigeria">human rights problems</a> included extrajudicial killings by security forces, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summary_executions" title="Summary executions">summary executions</a>; security force torture, rape, and other cruel, inhuman, or degrading treatment of prisoners, detainees, and criminal suspects; harsh and life-threatening prison and detention center conditions; arbitrary arrest and detention; prolonged pretrial detention; denial of fair public trial; executive influence on the judiciary; infringements on citizens' privacy rights; restrictions on freedom of speech, press, assembly, religion, and movement ..."</p>
<p>"On October 9, witnesses in Maiduguri claimed members of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Task_Force" title="Joint Task Force">JTF</a> "Restore Order", [a <a href="/wiki/Vigilante" title="Vigilante">vigilante</a> group] based in Maiduguri, went on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Killing_spree" title="Killing spree">killing spree</a> after a suspected Boko Haram bomb killed an officer. Media reported the JTF killed 20 to 45 civilians and razed 50 to 100 houses in the neighborhood. The JTF commander in Maiduguri denied the allegations. On November 2, witnesses claimed the JTF shot and killed up to 40 people during raids in Maiduguri. The army claimed it dismissed some officers from the military as a result of alleged abuses committed in Maiduguri, but there were no known formal prosecutions in Maiduguri by year's end."</p>
<p>"Credible reports also indicated ... uniformed military personnel and <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Mobile_Police" title="Nigerian Mobile Police">mobile police</a> carried out summary executions, assaults, torture, and other abuses throughout Bauchi, Borno, <a href="/wiki/Kano_State" title="Kano State">Kano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaduna_State" title="Kaduna State">Kaduna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plateau_State" title="Plateau State">Plateau</a>, and Yobe states ... The national police, army, and other security forces committed <a href="/wiki/Extrajudicial_killing" title="Extrajudicial killing">extrajudicial killings</a> and used lethal and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excessive_force" title="Excessive force">excessive force</a> to apprehend criminals and suspects, as well as to disperse protesters. Authorities generally did not hold police accountable for the use of excessive or <a href="/wiki/Deadly_force" title="Deadly force">deadly force</a> or for the deaths of persons <a href="/wiki/Death_in_custody" title="Death in custody">in custody</a>. Security forces generally operated with impunity in the illegal apprehension, detention, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Extrajudicial_punishment" title="Extrajudicial punishment">extrajudicial</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execution" title="Execution">execution</a> of criminal suspects. The reports of state or federal panels of inquiry investigating <a href="/wiki/Suspicious_death" title="Suspicious death">suspicious deaths</a> remained unpublished."</p>
<p>"There were no new developments in the case of five police officers accused of executing Muhammad Yusuf in 2009 at a state police headquarters. In July 2011 authorities arraigned five police officers in the federal high court in Abuja for the <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">murder</a> of Yusuf. The court granted bail to four of the officers, while one remained in custody."</p>
<p>"Police use of excessive force, including use of live ammunition, to disperse demonstrators resulted in numerous killings during the year. For example, although the January fuel subsidy demonstrations generally remained peaceful, security forces reportedly fired on protesters in various states across the country during those demonstrations, resulting in 10 to 15 deaths and an unknown number of wounded."</p>
<p>"Despite some improvements resulting from the closure of police checkpoints in many parts of the country, states with an increased security presence due to the activities of Boko Haram experienced a rise in violence and lethal force at police and military <a href="/wiki/Roadblock" title="Roadblock">roadblocks</a>."</p>
<p>"Continuing abductions of civilians by criminal groups occurred in the Niger Delta and <a href="/wiki/Biafra" title="Biafra">Southeast</a> ... Police and other security forces were often implicated in the kidnapping schemes."</p>
<p>"Although the constitution and law prohibit such practices and provide for punishment of such abuses, torture is not criminalized, and security service personnel, including police, military, and State Security Service (SSS) officers, regularly tortured, beat, and abused demonstrators, criminal suspects, detainees, and convicted prisoners. Police <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mistreatment" title="Mistreatment">mistreated</a> civilians to extort money. The law prohibits the introduction into trials of evidence and confessions obtained through torture; however, police often used torture to <a href="/wiki/Forced_confession" title="Forced confession">extract confessions</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2013">2013</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2013">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/2013_Baga_massacre" title="2013 Baga massacre">2013 Baga massacre</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lakechad_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="588" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Lakechad_map.png/220px-Lakechad_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Lakechad_map.png/330px-Lakechad_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Lakechad_map.png/440px-Lakechad_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lakechad_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Nigeria's Borno State, where Boko Haram is based, adjoins <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> as do <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> and the country of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>. The conflict and refugees spilled over the national borders to involve all four countries.</p>
<p>In 2013, Boko Haram increased operations in Northern Cameroon, and were involved in skirmishes along the borders of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> and Niger. They were linked to a number of kidnappings, often reportedly in association with the splinter group <a href="/wiki/Ansaru" title="Ansaru">Ansaru</a>, drawing towards them a higher level of international attention.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Counterterrorism" title="Bureau of Counterterrorism">U.S. Bureau of Counterterrorism</a> provides the following summary of Boko Haram's 2013 foreign operations:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In February 2013, Boko Haram was responsible for kidnapping seven French tourists in the far north of Cameroon. In November 2013, Boko Haram members kidnapped a French priest in Cameroon. In December 2013, Boko Haram gunmen reportedly attacked civilians in several areas of northern Cameroon. Security forces from Chad and Niger also reportedly partook in skirmishes against suspected Boko Haram members along Nigeria's borders. In 2013, the group also kidnapped eight French citizens in northern Cameroon and obtained ransom payments for their release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bureau_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bureau-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Boko Haram has often managed to evade the Nigerian army by retreating into the hills around the border with Cameroon, whose army is apparently unwilling to confront them. Nigeria, Chad and Niger had formed a Multinational Joint Task Force in 1998. In February 2012, Cameroon signed an agreement with Nigeria to establish a Joint Trans-Border Security Committee, which was inaugurated in November 2013, when Cameroon announced plans to conduct "coordinated but separate" border patrols in 2014. It convened again in July 2014 to further improve cooperation between the two countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 2013, Amnesty International received 'credible' information that over 950 inmates had died in custody, mostly in detention centres in Maiduguri and <a href="/wiki/Damaturu" title="Damaturu">Damaturu</a>, within the first half of the year. Official state corruption was also documented in December 2013 by the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">UK Home Office</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The NPF [Nigeria Police Force], SSS, and military report to civilian authorities; however, these security services periodically act outside of civilian control. The government lack effective mechanisms to investigate and punish abuse and corruption. The NPF remain susceptible to corruption, commit human rights abuses, and generally operate with impunity in the apprehension, illegal detention, and sometimes execution of criminal suspects. The SSS also commit human rights abuses, particularly in restricting freedom of speech and press. In some cases private citizens or the government brought charges against perpetrators of human rights abuses in these units. However, most cases lingered in court or went unresolved after an initial investigation.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The state of emergency was extended in May 2013 to cover the whole of the three north-eastern states of <a href="/wiki/Borno_State" title="Borno State">Borno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adamawa_State" title="Adamawa State">Adamawa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yobe_State" title="Yobe State">Yobe</a>, raising tensions in the region. In the 12 months following the announcement, 250,000 fled the three states, followed by a further 180,000 between May and August 2014. A further 210,000 fled from bordering states, bringing the total displaced by the conflict to 650,000. Many thousands left the country. An August 2014 Amnesty International video showed Army and allied militia executing people, including by slitting their throats, and dumping their corpses in mass graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> According to Human Rights Watch, more than 130 villages and towns were attacked or controlled by the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014">2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1023" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg/220px-Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg/330px-Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg/440px-Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelle-obama-bringbackourgirls.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a> raising public awareness of the Chibok kidnapping</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chibok_kidnapping">Chibok kidnapping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Chibok kidnapping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chibok_schoolgirls_kidnapping" title="Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping">Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping</a></div>
<p>In April 2014 Boko Haram kidnapped 276 schoolgirls from <a href="/wiki/Chibok" title="Chibok">Chibok</a>. Shekau announced his intention of selling them into slavery. More than 50 escaped. The incident brought Boko Haram extended global media attention, much of it focused on the pronouncements of the US <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady</a> Michelle Obama. Faced with condemnation for his perceived incompetence, as well as allegations from Amnesty International of state collusion, President Jonathan responded by hiring a Washington <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">PR</a> firm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Parents of the missing girls and those who had escaped were kept waiting until July to meet with the president, which caused them concern. In October, the government announced the girls' imminent release, but the information proved unreliable. The announcement to the media of a peace agreement and the imminent release of all the missing girls was followed days later by a video message in which Shekau stated that no such meeting had taken place and that the girls had been "married off". The announcement to the media, unaccompanied by any evidence of the reality of the agreement, was thought by analysts to have been a political ploy by the president to raise his popularity before his confirmation of his candidacy in the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_general_election,_2015" title="Nigerian general election, 2015">2015 general election</a>. Earlier in the year, the girls' plight had featured on "#BringBackOurGirls" political campaign posters in the streets of the capital, which the president denied knowledge of and soon took down after news of criticism surfaced. These posters, which were interpreted, to the dismay of campaigners for the girls' recapture, as being designed to benefit from the fame of the kidnapping, had also been part of Jonathan's "pre-presidential campaign". In September, "#BringBackGoodluck2015" campaign posters again drew criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The official announcement of the president's candidacy was made before cheering crowds in Abuja on 11 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2016, the organisations <a href="/wiki/International_Alert" title="International Alert">International Alert</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> published a study revealing that girls and women released from Boko Haram captivity often face rejection upon returning to their communities and families, in part due to a culture of stigma around sexual violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Cameroon">Northern Cameroon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Northern Cameroon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roumsiki.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="262" data-file-width="397" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Roumsiki.jpg/220px-Roumsiki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Roumsiki.jpg/330px-Roumsiki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Roumsiki.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roumsiki.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Northern Cameroon <a href="/wiki/Mandara_Mountains" title="Mandara Mountains">Mandara Mountains</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2014, Boko Haram continued to increase its presence in northern Cameroon. On May 16, ten Chinese workers were abducted in a raid on a construction company camp in Waza, near the Nigerian border. Vehicles and explosives were also taken in the raid, and one Cameroon soldier was killed. Cameroon's antiterrorist Rapid Intervention Battalion attempted to intervene but were vastly outnumbered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In July, the deputy prime minister's home village was attacked by around 200 militants; his wife was kidnapped, along with the Sultan of <a href="/wiki/Kolofata" title="Kolofata">Kolofata</a> and his family. At least 15 people, including soldiers and police, were killed in the raid. The deputy prime minister's wife was subsequently released in October, along with 26 others including the ten Chinese construction workers who had been captured in May; authorities made no comment about any ransom, which the Cameroon government had previously claimed it never pays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In a separate attack, nine bus passengers and a soldier were shot dead and the son of a local chief was kidnapped. Hundreds of local youths are suspected to have been recruited. In August, the remote Nigerian border town of <a href="/wiki/Gwoza" title="Gwoza">Gwoza</a> was overrun and held by the group. In response to the increased militant activity, the Cameroonian president sacked two senior military officers and sent his army chief with 1000 reinforcements to the northern border region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between May and July 2014, 8,000 Nigerian refugees arrived in the country, up to 25 percent suffering from acute malnutrition. Cameroon, which ranked 150 out of 186 on the 2012 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNDP" title="UNDP">UNDP</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">HDI</a>, hosted, as of August 2014, 107,000 refugees fleeing unrest in the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">CAR</a>, a number that was expected to increase to 180,000 by the end of the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> A further 11,000 Nigerian refugees crossed the border into Cameroon and Chad during August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_occupied_territory">Expansion of occupied territory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Expansion of occupied territory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="640" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg/220px-Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg/330px-Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg/440px-Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attentat_Nyanya_VOA2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wounded people following a <a href="/wiki/April_2014_Nyanya_bombing" title="April 2014 Nyanya bombing">bomb attack</a> by Boko Haram in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyanya" title="Nyanya">Nyanya</a>, in April 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The attack on Gwoza signalled a change in strategy for Boko Haram, as the group continued to capture territory in north-eastern and eastern areas of Borno, as well as in Adamawa and Yobe. Attacks across the border were repelled by the Cameroon military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The territorial gains were officially denied by the Nigerian military. In a video obtained by the news agency <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">AFP</a> on 24 August 2014, Shekau announced that Gwoza was now part of an Islamic <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bama,_Nigeria" title="Bama, Nigeria">Bama</a>, 70 kilometres (45 mi) from the state capital Maiduguri, was reported to have been captured at the beginning of September, resulting in thousands of residents fleeing to Maiduguri, even as residents there were themselves attempting to flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The military continued to deny Boko Haram's territorial gains, which were, however, confirmed by local vigilantes who had managed to escape. The militants were reportedly killing men and teenage boys in the town of over 250,000 inhabitants. Soldiers refused orders to advance on the occupied town; hundreds fled across the border into Cameroon, but were promptly repatriated. Fifty-four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deserter" title="Deserter">deserters</a> were later sentenced to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_by_firing_squad" title="Death by firing squad">death by firing squad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monica_Mark_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monica_Mark-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 October, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Defence_Staff_(Nigeria)" title="Chief of the Defence Staff (Nigeria)">Chief of the Defence Staff</a> announced that a ceasefire had been brokered, stating "I have accordingly directed the service chiefs to ensure immediate compliance with this development in the field." Despite a lack of confirmation from the militants, the announcement was publicised in newspaper headlines worldwide. Within 48 hours, however, the same publications were reporting that Boko Haram attacks had continued unabated. It was reported that factionalisation would make such a deal particularly difficult to achieve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="567" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png/220px-Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png/330px-Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png/440px-Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nigeria-karte-politisch-adamawa.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Location of the town of <a href="/wiki/Mubi" title="Mubi">Mubi</a> within <a href="/wiki/Adamawa_State" title="Adamawa State">Adamawa State</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 29 October, <a href="/wiki/Mubi" title="Mubi">Mubi</a>, a town of 200,000 in Adamawa, fell to the militants, further undermining confidence in the peace talks. Thousands fled south to Adamawa's capital city, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yola,_Nigeria" title="Yola, Nigeria">Yola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Amid media speculation that the ceasefire announcement had been part of President Jonathan's re-election campaign, a video statement released by Boko Haram through the normal communication channels via AFP on 31 October stated that no negotiations had in fact taken place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Mubi was said to have been recaptured by the army on 13 November. On the same day, Boko Haram seized Chibok; two days later, the army recaptured the largely deserted town. As of 16 November it was estimated that more than twenty towns and villages had been taken control of by the militants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> On 28 November, <a href="/wiki/2014_Kano_bombing" title="2014 Kano bombing">120 died</a> in an attack at the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kano" title="Great Mosque of Kano">central mosque</a> in Kano during Friday prayers. There were 27 Boko Haram attacks during the month of November, killing at least 786.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 3 December, it was reported that several towns in North Adamawa had been recovered by the Nigerian military with the help of local vigilantes. Bala Nggilari, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Adamawa_State" title="List of Governors of Adamawa State">governor of Adamawa state</a>, said that the military were aiming to recruit 4,000 vigilantes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC20141203_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20141203-160">[160]</a></sup> On 13 December, Boko Haram attacked the village of Gumsuri in Borno, killing over 30, and kidnapping over 100 women and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monica_Mark_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monica_Mark-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_in_Cameroon">Attacks in Cameroon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Attacks in Cameroon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/December_2014_Cameroon_clashes" title="December 2014 Cameroon clashes">December 2014 Cameroon clashes</a></div>
<p>In the second half of December, the focus of activity switched to the <a href="/wiki/Far_North_Region_(Cameroon)" title="Far North Region (Cameroon)">Far North Region</a> of Cameroon, beginning on the morning of 17 December when an army convoy was attacked with an IED and ambushed by hundreds of militants near the border town of <a href="/wiki/Amchide" title="Amchide">Amchide</a>, 60 kilometres (40 mi) north of the state capital <a href="/wiki/Maroua" title="Maroua">Maroua</a>. One soldier was confirmed dead, and an estimated 116 militants were killed in the attack, which was followed by another attack overnight with unknown casualties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> On 22 December the Rapid Intervention Battalion followed up with an attack on a Boko Haram training camp near Guirdivig, arresting 45 militants and seizing 84 children aged 7–15 who were undergoing training, according to a statement from Cameroon's <a href="/wiki/List_of_ministries_of_Cameroon" title="List of ministries of Cameroon">Ministry of Defense</a>. The militants fled in pick-up trucks carrying an unknown number of their dead; no information on army casualties was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> On 27–28 December five villages were simultaneously attacked, and for the first time the Cameroon military launched air attacks when Boko Haram briefly occupied an army camp. Casualty figures were not released. According to Information Minister Issa Tchiroma,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Units of the group attacked Makari, Amchide, Limani and Achigachia in a change of strategy which consists of distracting Cameroonian troops on different fronts, making them more vulnerable in the face of the mobility and unpredictability of their attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2015">2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 2015">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Baga_massacre">Baga massacre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Baga massacre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/2015_Baga_massacre" title="2015 Baga massacre">2015 Baga massacre</a></div>
<p>On 3 January 2015 Boko Haram attacked <a href="/wiki/Baga,_Nigeria" title="Baga, Nigeria">Baga</a>, seizing it and the multinational joint task force military base. As the militants advanced the army fled. Some residents managed to escape to Chad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Although the death toll of the massacre was earlier estimated by <a href="/wiki/Western_media" title="Western media">western media</a> to be upwards of 2000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> the Defence Ministry dismissed these claims as "speculation and conjecture", estimating the figure to be closer to 150.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> On 25 January the militants advanced to <a href="/wiki/Monguno" title="Monguno">Monguno</a>, capturing the town and a nearby military base. Their advance on Maiduguri and <a href="/wiki/Konduga" title="Konduga">Konduga</a>, 40 km to the southeast, was repelled. After retaking Monguno, the army expelled the militants from Baga on 21 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Baga massacre was one of the Nigerian army's biggest defeats in terms of loss of equipment and civilian casualties. Several officers were <a href="/wiki/Court-martial" title="Court-martial">court-martialed</a>. In October 2015 General <a href="/wiki/Enitan_Ransome-Kuti" title="Enitan Ransome-Kuti">Enitan Ransome-Kuti</a> was dismissed from the army and sentenced to six months imprisonment. It was determined that he had failed in his duty to launch a counter-attack after retreating from the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_African_offensive">West African offensive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: West African offensive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2015_West_African_offensive" title="2015 West African offensive">2015 West African offensive</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram,_mars_2015..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="640" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram%2C_mars_2015..jpg/220px-Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram%2C_mars_2015..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram%2C_mars_2015..jpg/330px-Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram%2C_mars_2015..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram%2C_mars_2015..jpg/440px-Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram%2C_mars_2015..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Militaire_nig%C3%A9riens_contre_Boko_Haram,_mars_2015..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Niger_Armed_Forces" title="Niger Armed Forces">Niger Armed Forces</a> fighting Boko Haram in March 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Starting in late January 2015, a coalition of military forces from Nigeria, Chad, Cameroon, and Niger began a campaign against Boko Haram. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> On 4 February, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Chad" title="Military of Chad">Chad Army</a> killed over 200 Boko Haram militants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Soon afterwards, Boko Haram launched an attack on the Cameroonian town of <a href="/wiki/Fotokol" title="Fotokol">Fotokol</a>, killing 81 civilians, 13 Chadian soldiers and 6 Cameroonian soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
