<p>On 7 March 2015, Boko Haram's leader Abubakar Shekau pledged allegiance to ISIL via an audio message posted on the organisation's Twitter account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BokoHaramIS2_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-BokoHaramIS2-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Nigerian army spokesperson Sami Usman Kukasheka said the pledge was a sign of weakness and that Shekau was like a "drowning man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> On 12 March 2015, ISIL's spokesman <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mohammad_al-Adnani" title="Abu Mohammad al-Adnani">Abu Mohammad al-Adnani</a> released an audiotape in which he welcomed the pledge of allegiance, and described it as an expansion of the group's caliphate to West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BokoHaramIS_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-BokoHaramIS-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 March 2015, residents of <a href="/wiki/Damasak" title="Damasak">Damasak</a>, Nigeria said that Boko Haram had taken more than 400 women and children from the town as they fled from coalition forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> On March 27, the Nigerian army captured Gwoza, which was believed to be the location of Boko Haram headquarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> On election day, 28 March 2015, Boko Haram extremists killed 41 people, including a legislator, to discourage hundreds from voting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2015, Boko Haram lost control of the Northern Nigerian towns of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bama,_Nigeria" title="Bama, Nigeria">Bama</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCTide_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCTide-177">[177]</a></sup> and Gwoza<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> to the Nigerian army. The Nigerian authorities said that they had taken back 11 of the 14 districts previously controlled by Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCTide_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCTide-177">[177]</a></sup> In April, four Boko Haram camps in the <a href="/wiki/Sambisa_Forest" title="Sambisa Forest">Sambisa Forest</a> were overrun by the Nigerian military who freed nearly 300 females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Boko Haram forces were believed to have retreated to the <a href="/wiki/Mandara_Mountains" title="Mandara Mountains">Mandara Mountains</a>, along the Nigeria-Cameroon border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attrition_of_Catholic_diocese_of_Maiduguri">Attrition of Catholic diocese of Maiduguri</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Attrition of Catholic diocese of Maiduguri">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A report by the Catholic diocese of Maiduguri estimated that, as of May 2015, over 5,000 Nigerian Catholics had been killed by Boko Haram. The diocese also reported 7,000 widows and 10,000 orphans among its laity. Furthermore, Boko Haram militants had taken over several parish centres within the diocese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="July_mosques_massacres">July mosques massacres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: July mosques massacres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/July_2015_Kukawa,_Nigeria_Massacre" title="July 2015 Kukawa, Nigeria Massacre">July 2015 Kukawa, Nigeria Massacre</a></div>
<p>Boko Haram militants attacked multiple mosques between 1 and 2 July. Forty-eight men and boys were killed on the 1st at one mosque in <a href="/wiki/Kukawa" title="Kukawa">Kukawa</a>. Seventeen were wounded in the attack. Ninety-seven others, mostly men, were killed in numerous mosques on the 2nd with a number of women and young girls killed in their homes. An unknown number were wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suicide_bombings_in_Chad">Suicide bombings in Chad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Suicide bombings in Chad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 15 June 2015, two suicide bombings of police sites in <a href="/wiki/N%27Djamena" title="N'Djamena">N'Djamena</a>, the capital and largest city of Chad, killed 38 people. Boko Haram later claimed responsibility for these attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-juneattack_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-juneattack-183">[183]</a></sup> On 11 July, a male suicide bomber disguised in a woman's burqa detonated his explosives belt in the main market of N'Djamena, next to the main mosque, killing 15 people and injuring 80.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Several days after the bombing, Boko Haram claimed responsibility via Twitter, signing as "Islamic State, West Africa province."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_defeat">Claims of defeat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Claims of defeat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_general_election,_2015" title="Nigerian general election, 2015">March 2015 general election</a> was won by Buhari, who had vowed to remove inefficiency and corruption in the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> On 9 September 2015 the Director of Information at the Defence Headquarters, Colonel Rabe Abubakar announced that all known Boko Haram camps and cells had been destroyed, and that the group was so weakened that they could no longer hold any territory:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>These terrorists have been subdued, even if they are adopting other means and as they are re-strategising, we are also doing the same and pre-empting them. We have coordinated the air and ground assaults to make sure that these terrorists' hideouts are completely decimated. As I am speaking to you, all the terrorists’ camps have completely been wiped out. So right now they are completely in disarray, have no command and control of where to plan. We have even taken over their camps that most of them abandoned and are attempting to blend into towns and communities. We have also apprehended some of them and very soon innocent Nigerians can move back to their communities. We are making a lot of headway, so people should know that Boko Haram is no longer strong enough to hold grounds. Very soon this issue of whether they are in control of any territory in Nigeria or not will come to the open. I am assuring you that they will never again recapture the territory taken from them because what is happening right now with the deployment of troops, equipment and morale will ensure that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defeat_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-defeat-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Buhari later reiterated, in December 2015, that Boko Haram was "technically defeated",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCDefeated_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCDefeated-188">[188]</a></sup> and declared in December 2016 that the group had been entirely ousted from its last stronghold of Sambisa Forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Borno">Borno</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Borno">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/September_2015_Borno_State_bombings" title="September 2015 Borno State bombings">September 2015 Borno State bombings</a></div>
<p>On 20 September a series of bombings occurred in Maiduguri and Monguno. The attacks followed an announcement by Shekau refuting the army's claims of defeat. A military spokesman stated that the event showed the "high level of desperation" of Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> The Arewa Consultative Forum released a statement condemning the bombings and commending the military offensive:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The ACF condemns in strong terms the continued use of suicide bombers by Boko Haram terrorists to kill innocent people in the name of a religious war, as no religion condones such cruel and barbaric act. The ACF wishes to commend the military and other security agencies for the continued onslaught on the terrorists’ enclaves and hideouts, thereby dislodging them from their strong holds. The ACF urges the military not to be deterred by the cowardly act of the Boko Haram terrorists, as their renewed effort and determination will soon end the insurgency. The ACF also appeals to the military to intensify its synergy of sharing intelligence with the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On 21 October in <a href="/wiki/Nganzai" title="Nganzai">Nganzai</a>, Borno, according to a civilian vigilante, fleeing militants shot at four cars, killing the passengers, and burnt and looted the nearby village.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> On 23 October a suicide bombing occurred in a pre dawn attack at a mosque in Maiduguri. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=National_Emergency_Management_Agency_(Nigeria)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="National Emergency Management Agency (Nigeria) (page does not exist)">National Emergency Management Agency</a> (NEMA) put the death toll at 6, while hospital sources reported 19 deaths, and a vigilante claimed to have counted 28 corpses and two suicide bombers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> On the following day four female suicide bombers claimed one victim after they were intercepted by the JTF in Maiduguri, according to a NEMA spokesman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 27 October a military operation freed 192 children and 138 women being held captive in two camps in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sambisa" title="Sambisa">Sambisa</a> forest, and 30 militants were killed, according to a social media statement from the Defense HQ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> None of the captives were those taken in Chibok in April 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-general_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-general-197">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 December gunmen set fire to the village of Kimba, killing at least 14, according to vigilantes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> On 27 December gunmen armed with <a href="/wiki/Rocket-propelled_grenade" title="Rocket-propelled grenade">RPGs</a> battled with troops for two hours in Aldawari village in the outskirts of Maiduguri, according to NEMA. On the following morning a bombing at a nearby mosque killed around 20, according to NEMA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dec28_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dec28-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_Capital_Territory_.2F_Nasarawa">Federal Capital Territory / Nasarawa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Federal Capital Territory / Nasarawa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Two bombings on 2 October that killed 18 and wounded 41, one in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyanya" title="Nyanya">Nyanya</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasarawa" title="Nasarawa">Nasarawa</a> and the other in <a href="/wiki/Kuje" title="Kuje">Kuje</a>, FCT, were also claimed by Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adamawa">Adamawa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Adamawa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 1 October villagers in Kirchinga, Adamawa complained of a lack of security personnel after 5 residents had their throats slit during an unchallenged early morning attack. The village borders Cameroon and the Sambisa forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> On 18 October the village of Dar, Adamawe was attacked. Maina Ularamu, a former Chairman of <a href="/wiki/Madagali" title="Madagali">Madagali</a> Local Government Area, stated: "A large number of gunmen invaded the village, forcing residents to flee to a nearby bush. Two female suicide bombers disguised as fleeing villagers detonated explosives in the bush where many people were hiding, killing 12 persons."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> On 20 October there were reports of a military ambush in Madagali, assisted by vigilantes, in which over 30 militants were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> On 21 October, according to vigilante reports, a joint operation in Madagali and Gwoza killed 150 militants and rescued 36 captives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> On 23 October a suicide bomb at a crowded mosque killed 27 in Yola, Adamawa's capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chicago_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chicago-208">[208]</a></sup> On 17 November an explosion at a food market in Yola killed 32,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> in the first Nigerian bombing since the 23 October attacks in Maiduguri and Yola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> On the morning of 28 December two female suicide bombers detonated their explosives at a crowded market in Madagali. According to a local resident at least 28 were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dec28_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dec28-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yobe">Yobe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Yobe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 7 October in <a href="/wiki/Damaturu" title="Damaturu">Damaturu</a>, Yobe at least 15 people were killed by 3 suicide bombers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> In Goniri, Yobe 7 soldiers and over 100 militants were killed, and a large arms cache was found, according to an army spokesman, who said that the recent apparent rise in suicide bombings was an indication of the success of military operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kano">Kano</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Kano">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Boko Haram claimed responsibility for a suicide attack on a procession of Shi'ite Muslims killing at least 21, on 30 November near the village of Dakozoye. A week earlier two bombers had killed at least 14 in Kano city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cameroon">Cameroon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Cameroon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 12 January, Boko Haram attacked a Cameroon military base in <a href="/wiki/Kolofata" title="Kolofata">Kolofata</a>. Government forces report killing 143 militants, while one Cameroon soldier was killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> On 18 January Boko Haram raided two Tourou Cameroon area villages, torching houses, killing some residents and kidnapping between 60 and 80 people including an estimated 50 young children between the ages of 10 and 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 October in the far north region of Cameroon two female suicide bombers killed nine people in the town of Mora. On 18 October 10 militants were killed when they attacked a Cameroon military anti-terrorist division convoy close to the border, after a military vehicle became stuck in mud. One army commander later died of his wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> On 12 October the first 90 of a proposed deployment of 300 US troops arrived in the region to assist with training, reconnaissance and airborne intelligence using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_drones" title="Predator drones">Predator drones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> On 16 October more than six security vehicles were transferred to the Cameroon military. An <a href="/wiki/United_States_Africa_Command" title="United States Africa Command">AFRICOM</a> spokesman said that increased cooperation had led them "to study the viability of ISR flights from a temporary location in Cameroon." The deployment is "totally separate and distinct" from operations in Chad and Niger, where 250 and 85 personnel, respectively, are conducting missions including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intelligence,_surveillance_and_reconnaissance" title="Intelligence, surveillance and reconnaissance">ISR</a> and training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 23 October Boko Haram fighters were driven out of Kerawa, a village of 50,000 in <a href="/wiki/Kolofata" title="Kolofata">Kolofata</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Communes_of_Cameroon" title="Communes of Cameroon">commune</a> in the far north region. They had briefly occupied the village until the arrival of security forces. Reports of civilian casualties ranged from eight to eleven. An army spokesman claimed the militants suffered heavy casualties. The village's military base had previously been targeted by suicide bombers on 3 September, when 30 were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 9 November two female suicide bombers killed three Nigerians during a security check in a truck full of Nigerian refugees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> On 21 November a suicide attack in a suburb of <a href="/wiki/Fotokol" title="Fotokol">Fotokol</a> town killed four. An anonymous military official said: "The first kamikaze detonated his bomb in the house of the traditional chief of Leymarie. Five people died including the bomber. Several minutes later, three female bombers exploded their bombs close to the initial site but they didn't kill anyone else because they acted too quickly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 28 November two suicide bombers killed six near the military base in Dabanga, and in an attack in Gouzoudou five people were killed, according to a military spokesman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> On 1 December two suicide bombers killed three, and a third bomber was killed before detonating explosives. On 2 December Cameroon's Defense Minister claimed that, at the end of November, 100 Boko Haram members had been killed and 900 hostages freed, and that a large stockpile of arms and munitions, and black-and-white ISIL flags had been seized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Information Minister Issa Tchiroma Bakari said that "The people that were freed are just villagers. The [Chibok] schoolgirls who are missing are not amongst the group."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chad">Chad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Chad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 6 October the Chadian army reported an attack in the border region of Lake Chad. 11 soldiers were killed and 14 wounded in the pre dawn cross-border infiltration, and 17 militants were also killed, according to an army spokesman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> On 10 October 5 suicide bombers killed 33 in the market in Baga Sola, a camp for Nigerian refugees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> On 1 November two dawn attacks on army posts occurred. Eleven militants and two soldiers were killed at Kaika, and in an attempted suicide bombing at Bougouma, "Two members of Boko Haram were neutralised and a third blew himself up, wounding 11 civilians", according to a government statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> A state of emergency was imposed in the western Lake Chad region on 9 November, initially for 12 days, and extended by Chad's national assembly on 18 November to four months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 December 2015 three female suicide bombers killed about 30 at a crowded market on the island of Koulfoua in Lake Chad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Niger">Niger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Niger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 25 September at least 15 civilians were massacred and stores were looted in a cross-border raid on a Niger village, according to anonymous military sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> On 2 October two soldiers died and four were wounded in a Boko Haram attack on a village near the Nigerian border in Niger's <a href="/wiki/Diffa_Region" title="Diffa Region">Diffa</a> province. The militants also looted stores, according to Niger army officers. On 4 October, according to an aid worker, a policeman and five civilians were killed by 4 suicide bombers near the Nigerian border. On 6 October three suspected Boko Haram militants accidentally blew themselves up while transporting explosives to <a href="/wiki/Bosso,_Niger" title="Bosso, Niger">Bosso</a> town in Diffa. On 21 October near Diffa town two soldiers were killed by explosives while intercepting an attack. Diffa region hosts over 150,000 Nigerian refugees. It is under a state of emergency. On 14 October a curfew and movement restrictions were imposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> At least 57 attacks occurred there from February to October 2015. More than 1,100 Boko Haram suspects have been arrested in Niger during 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 November two Niger military officials described an attack on a village in Bosso district in which five civilians and 20 militants were killed. A senior government official later denied that the attack had occurred, according to <i>Reuters</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> On 26 November Boko Haram launched a cross-border night raid on Wogom village in Diffa province. A government spokesman, Justice Minister Marou Amadou stated: "Eighteen villagers were killed, including the chief imam for the village whose throat was slit by his own nephew."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2016">2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: 2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dalori_attack">Dalori attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Dalori attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/2016_Dalori_attack" title="2016 Dalori attack">2016 Dalori attack</a></div>
<p>On 30 January 2016, at least 86 people were killed and at least 62 more injured in an attack by Boko Haram militants on Dalori Village which is located 4 kilometers from <a href="/wiki/Maiduguri" title="Maiduguri">Maiduguri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Nigerian Army</a> was unable to fight the militants until reinforcements arrived, causing Boko Haram to retreat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aljazeera_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Weakening_and_split">Weakening and split</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Weakening and split">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> figures indicated that Boko Haram killed 244 people in the second quarter of 2016 - the lowest quarterly figure for five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early August 2016, ISIL announced that it had appointed Abu-Musab al-Barnawi as the new leader of the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnawi_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barnawi-1">[1]</a></sup> In a video released a few days later, Shekau refused to accept al-Barnawi's appointment as leader and vowed to fight him while stating that he was still loyal to ISIL's leader Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vows_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vows-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The group has since split into pro-Barnawi and pro-Shekau factions, with reports of armed clashes breaking out between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clash_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clash-3">[3]</a></sup> Shekau has released videos since the split in which he refers to his group by its previous name of <i>Jamatu Ahlis Sunna Lidawatti wal Jihad</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 August 2016, Major General Lucky Irabor stated that the militants now only controlled a few villages and towns near Lake Chad and in Sambisa forest. He further stated that the military expected recapturing the final strongholds of the group within weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaders.2C_structure_and_members">Leaders, structure and members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Leaders, structure and members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although Boko Haram is organized in a hierarchical structure with one overall leader, the group also operates as a <a href="/wiki/Clandestine_cell_system" title="Clandestine cell system">clandestine cell system</a> using a network structure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-START_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-START-249">[249]</a></sup> with units having between 300 and 500 fighters each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> Estimates of the total number of fighters range between 500 and 9,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conflict-news.com_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-conflict-news.com-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visionofhumanity.org-p53_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-visionofhumanity.org-p53-252">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 2002 and 2009, Boko Haram was led by the organization founder, Mohammed Yusuf. In 2009, after Yusuf was killed, leadership passed to Abubakar Shekau, who was Yusuf's second-in command. Abubakar Shekau was born in Yobe, Nigeria between 1965 and 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCTC_Shekau_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCTC_Shekau-254">[254]</a></sup> He speaks Arabic, Hausa, Fulani, and Kanuri. On 21 June 2012 the U.S. Department of State designated Abubakar Shekau a Specially Designated Global Terrorist. On August 3, 2016, Abu Musab al-Barnawi, who was previously spokesman for Boko Haram was named new leader of the group by the Islamic State, to which it is affiliated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCTC_Shekau_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCTC_Shekau-254">[254]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financing">Financing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Financing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kidnappings.2C_robbery_and_extortion">Kidnappings, robbery and extortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Kidnappings, robbery and extortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Boko Haram gets funding from bank robberies and kidnapping ransoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIKE_OBOH_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIKE_OBOH-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup> As an example, in early 2013, gunmen from Boko Haram kidnapped a family of seven French tourists on vacation in Cameroon. Two months later, the kidnappers released the hostages along with 16 others in exchange for a ransom of $3.15 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caulderwood_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caulderwood-256">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>Any funding they may have received in the past from al-Qaeda affiliates is insignificant compared to the estimated $1 million ransom for each wealthy Nigerian or foreigner kidnapped. Cash is moved around by couriers, making it impossible to track, and communication is conducted face-to-face. Their mode of operation, which is thought to include paying local youths to track army movements, is such that little funding is required to carry out attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> Equipment captured from fleeing soldiers keeps the group constantly well-supplied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> The group also extorts local governments. A spokesman of Boko Haram claimed that Kano state governor <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Shekarau" title="Ibrahim Shekarau">Ibrahim Shekarau</a> and Bauchi state governor <a href="/wiki/Isa_Yuguda" title="Isa Yuguda">Isa Yuguda</a> had paid them monthly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thenation20120129_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-thenation20120129-259">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247-20120123_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-247-20120123-260">[260]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Donations_from_Islamist_sympathizers_and_others">Donations from Islamist sympathizers and others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Donations from Islamist sympathizers and others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After Boko Haram was founded, it was suspected of receiving funds from corrupt local politicians to discredit their opponents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamb_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lamb-72">[72]</a></sup> and from local donors who supported its goal of imposing Islamic law while ridding Nigeria of Western influences. In more recent times, Boko Haram has broadened its funding by drawing on foreign donors, and other ventures such as fake charity organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caulderwood_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caulderwood-256">[256]</a></sup> In February 2012, recently arrested officials revealed that while the organization initially relied on donations from members, its links with Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb opened it up to funding from groups in Saudi Arabia and the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Funding_Sources_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Funding_Sources-261">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boko Haram cloaks its sources of finance through the use of a highly decentralized distribution network. The group employs an Islamic model of money transfer called <a href="/wiki/Hawala" title="Hawala">hawala</a>, which is based on an honor system and a global network of agents that makes the financing difficult to track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caulderwood_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-Caulderwood-256">[256]</a></sup> In the past, Nigerian officials have been criticized for being unable to trace much of the funding that Boko Haram has received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gives_Notice_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gives_Notice-263">[263]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Drug_trafficking">Drug trafficking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Drug trafficking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Boko Haram has occasionally been connected in media reports with cocaine trafficking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> James Cockayne, formerly Co-Director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_on_Global_Counterterrorism_Cooperation" title="Center on Global Counterterrorism Cooperation">Center on Global Counterterrorism Cooperation</a> and Senior Fellow at the <a href="/wiki/International_Peace_Institute" title="International Peace Institute">International Peace Institute</a>, wrote in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Given their appreciation of the contested nature of much African governance, it comes as something of a surprise that Carrier and Klantschnig [<i>Review of Africa and the War on Drugs</i>, 2012] fiercely downplay the impact that cocaine trafficking is having on West African governance. On the basis of just three case studies (Guinea-Bissau, Lesotho and Nigeria) the authors conclude that 'state complicity' in the African drug trade is 'rare', and the dominant paradigm is 'repression'. As a result, they radically understate the close involvement of political and military actors in drug trafficking – particularly in West African cocaine trafficking – and overlook the growing power of drug money in African electoral politics, local and traditional governance, and security.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ties_to_other_militant_groups">Ties to other militant groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Ties to other militant groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, letters from Boko Haram were reportedly found in bin Laden's compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thedailybeast.com_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-thedailybeast.com-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three weeks after the July 2009 riots, AQIM expressed sympathy for Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-269">[269]</a></sup></p>
<p>Speaking by phone to reporters in November 2012, group spokesman <a href="/wiki/Abu_Qaqa" title="Abu Qaqa">Abu Qaqa</a> said: "We are together with al-Qaeda, they are promoting the cause of Islam, just as we are doing. Therefore they help us in our struggle and we help them, too." The 2012 Reuters special report details how fighters have trained with al-Qaeda affiliates in small groups over at least 6 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the UN Security Council listing of Boko Haram under the al-Qaeda sanctions regime in May 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> the group "has maintained a relationship with <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_the_Islamic_Maghreb" title="Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb">AQIM</a> for training and material support purposes", and "gained valuable knowledge on the construction of improvised explosive devices from AQIM". The UN found that a "number of Boko Haram members fought alongside al Qaeda affiliated groups in Mali in 2012 and 2013 before returning to Nigeria with terrorist expertise". AQIM is one of al-Qaeda's regional branches, whose leader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Musab_Abdel_Wadoud" title="Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud">Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud</a>, has sworn an oath of allegiance to al-Qaeda's senior leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longwarjournal.org_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-longwarjournal.org-271">[271]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, al-Qaeda Central has never officially accepted Boko Haram as an affiliate, and after the <a href="/wiki/Chibok_schoolgirls_kidnapping" title="Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping">Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping</a>, al-Qaeda did not praise Boko Haram, leading some analysts to conclude that the group was too violent for al-Qaeda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-269">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> The form and structure of al-Qaeda and its affiliates remains a matter of debate within the US Intelligence Community, and the exact current status of ties between Boko Haram and the al-Qaeda organization remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thedailybeast.com_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-thedailybeast.com-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2014, Shekau released a 16-minute video where he voiced support for <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a>'s head <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi">Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi</a>, al-Qaeda's head <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a> and Afghan Taliban leader <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mullah Omar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> In March 2015, Shekau formally pledged allegiance to ISIL, which was accepted by the group's spokesman several days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BokoHaramIS_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-BokoHaramIS-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2015 US military officials noted that while Boko Haram had improved their use of propaganda, there is no evidence of on-the-ground ISIL advisors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-general_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-general-197">[197]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Response_by_Nigerian_authorities">Response by Nigerian authorities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Response by Nigerian authorities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Nigerian military is, in the words of a former British military attaché speaking in 2014, "a shadow of what it's reputed to have once been. It's fallen apart." They are short of basic equipment, including radios and <a href="/wiki/Armoured_fighting_vehicle" title="Armoured fighting vehicle">armoured vehicles</a>. Morale is said to be low. Senior officers are alleged to be skimming military procurement budget funds that are intended to pay for the standard issue equipment of soldiers. The country's defense budget accounts for more than a third of the security budget of $5.8 billion, but only 10 percent is allocated to capital spending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> In a 2014 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a> assessment, funds are being "skimmed off the top", troops are "showing signs of real fear", and are "afraid to even engage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Congressional_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Congressional-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup></p>
<p>In the summer of 2013, the Nigerian military shut down mobile phone coverage in the three north-eastern states to disrupt the group's communication and ability to detonate IEDs. Accounts from military insiders and data of Boko Haram incidences before, during and after the mobile phone blackout suggest that the shut down was 'successful' from a military- tactical point of view. However it angered citizens in the region (owing to negative social and economic consequences of the mobile shutdown) and engendered negative opinions toward the state and new emergency policies. While citizens and organizations developed various coping and circumventing strategies, Boko Haram evolved from an open network model of insurgency to a closed centralized system, shifting the center of its operations to the <a href="/wiki/Sambisa_Forest" title="Sambisa Forest">Sambisa Forest</a>. This fundamentally changed the dynamics of the conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2014, Nigeria was estimated to have had the highest number of terrorist killings in the world over the past year, 3477, killed in 146 attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup> The governor of Borno, <a href="/wiki/Kashim_Shettima" title="Kashim Shettima">Kashim Shettima</a>, of the opposition <a href="/wiki/All_Nigeria_Peoples_Party" title="All Nigeria Peoples Party">ANPP</a>, said in February 2014:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Boko Haram are better armed and are better motivated than our own troops. Given the present state of affairs, it is absolutely impossible for us to defeat Boko Haram.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In March 2015, it was reported that Nigeria had employed hundreds of mercenaries from South Africa and the former Soviet Union to assist in making gains against Boko Haram before the March 28 election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2015, General <a href="/wiki/David_M._Rodriguez" title="David M. Rodriguez">David M. Rodriguez</a>, head of U.S. Africa Command, reported that Boko Haram has lost territory, directly contradicting statements made by Boko Haram. U.S. efforts to train and share intelligence with regional military forces is credited with helping to push back against Boko Haram, but officials warn that the group remains a grave threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_responses">International responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: International responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_responses">United States responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: United States responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2012, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State" title="U.S. Department of State">U.S. Department of State</a> had an internal debate on whether to place Boko Haram on its <a href="/wiki/United_States_State_Department_list_of_Foreign_Terrorist_Organizations" title="United States State Department list of Foreign Terrorist Organizations">list of Foreign Terrorist Organizations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KesslerBoko_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-KesslerBoko-288">[288]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Counterterrorism" title="Bureau of Counterterrorism">Bureau of Counterterrorism</a> leaned towards designation while the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_African_Affairs" title="Bureau of African Affairs">Bureau of African Affairs</a> urged caution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KesslerBoko_288-1"><a href="#cite_note-KesslerBoko-288">[288]</a></sup> Officials from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a>, the FBI, the CIA, and a number of members of Congress urged the State Department to designate Boko Haram as an FTO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> The Nigerian government voiced its opposition to an FTO designation, citing concerns that it would raise Boko Haram's stature and have implications for humanitarian aid in the region where Boko Haram operated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KesslerBoko_288-2"><a href="#cite_note-KesslerBoko-288">[288]</a></sup> Twenty academic experts on Nigeria signed a letter to the State Department urging it not to designate Boko Haram as an FTO, saying that it would hinder NGO efforts in the region and might legitimize the Nigerian Army's human rights abuses in its efforts to fight Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KesslerBoko_288-3"><a href="#cite_note-KesslerBoko-288">[288]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. State Department designated Boko Haram and its offshoot <a href="/wiki/Ansaru" title="Ansaru">Ansaru</a> as terrorist organizations in November 2013, citing Boko Haram's links with AQIM and its responsibility for "thousands of deaths in northeast and central Nigeria over the last several years including targeted killings of civilians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office_of_the_Spokesperson_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-Office_of_the_Spokesperson-292">[292]</a></sup> The State Department also cited Ansaru's 2013 kidnapping and execution of seven international construction workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office_of_the_Spokesperson_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-Office_of_the_Spokesperson-292">[292]</a></sup> In the statement it was noted, however, "These designations are an important and appropriate step, but only one tool in what must be a comprehensive approach by the Nigerian government to counter these groups through a combination of law enforcement, political, and development efforts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office_of_the_Spokesperson_292-2"><a href="#cite_note-Office_of_the_Spokesperson-292">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup> The State Department had resisted earlier calls to designate Boko Haram as a terrorist group after the <a href="/wiki/2011_Abuja_United_Nations_bombing" title="2011 Abuja United Nations bombing">2011 Abuja United Nations bombing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> The U.S. government does not believe Boko Haram is currently (2014) affiliated with al Qaeda Central, despite regular periodic pledges of support and solidarity from its leadership for al-Qaeda, but is particularly concerned about ties between Boko Haram and Al Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb (AQIM) (including "likely sharing funds, training, and explosive materials").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Congressional_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Congressional-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Efforts to cooperate in freeing the Chibok schoolgirls had faltered, largely due to mutual distrust; the infiltration of the military by Boko Haram meant that U.S. officials were wary of sharing raw intelligence data, and the Nigerian military had failed to supply information that might have aided U.S. drone flights in locating the kidnapped girls. The Nigerian government claims that Boko Haram is "the West Africa branch of the world-wide Al-Qaida movement" with connections to <a href="/wiki/Al-Shabaab_(militant_group)" title="Al-Shabaab (militant group)">al-Shabaab</a> in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> and AQIM in Mali. The Nigerian government denies having committed human rights abuses in the conflict, and therefore oppose U.S. restrictions on arms sales, which they see as being based on the U.S. mis-application of the <a href="/wiki/Leahy_Law" title="Leahy Law">Leahy Law</a> due to concerns over <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Nigeria" title="Human rights in Nigeria">human rights in Nigeria</a>. The U.S. had supplied the Nigerian army with trucks and equipment but had blocked the sale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobra_helicopter" title="Cobra helicopter">Cobra helicopters</a>. In November 2014 the U.S. State department again refused to supply Cobras, citing concerns over the Nigerian military's ability to maintain and use them without endangering civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cobra_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-cobra-298">[298]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 December 2014 the U.S. embassy in Abuja announced that the U.S. had discontinued training a Nigerian battalion at the request of the Nigerian government. A spokesman for the U.S. state department said, "We regret premature termination of this training, as it was to be the first in a larger planned project that would have trained additional units with the goal of helping the Nigerian Army build capacity to counter Boko Haram. The U.S. government will continue other aspects of the extensive bilateral security relationship, as well as all other assistance programs, with Nigeria. The U.S. government is committed to the long tradition of partnership with Nigeria and will continue to engage future requests for cooperation and training."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cobra_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-cobra-298">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 September 2015 the White House announced a military aid package for African allies fighting Boko Haram. The package included up to $45 million for training and other support for Benin, Cameroon, Chad, Niger and Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> On 14 October the White House released a statement, in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/War_Powers_Resolution" title="War Powers Resolution">War Powers Resolution</a>, announcing the deployment of 300 troops to Cameroon to conduct airborne ISR: "These forces are equipped with weapons for the purpose of providing their own force protection and security, and they will remain in Cameroon until their support is no longer needed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_Coalition_force">African Coalition force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: African Coalition force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After a series of meetings over many months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngrguardiannews.com_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-ngrguardiannews.com-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sunnewsonline.com_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-sunnewsonline.com-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-13">[13]</a></sup> Cameroon's foreign minister announced on 30 November 2014 that a coalition force to fight terrorism, including Boko Haram, would soon be operational. The force would include 3,500 soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, Chad, Cameroon, Niger and Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup> Discussions between the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a> (ECOWAS) about a broader based military force have been scheduled.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2015)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_and_British_assistance">French and British assistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: French and British assistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>France and the UK, in coordination with the United States, have sent trainers, and material assistance to Nigeria to assist in the fight against Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmp.com_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-scmp.com-305">[305]</a></sup> France planned to use 3,000 troops in the region for counter-terrorism operations. Israel and Canada also pledged support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_assistance">Chinese assistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Chinese assistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In May 2014, China offered Nigeria assistance that included satellite data, and possibly military equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scmp.com_305-1"><a href="#cite_note-scmp.com-305">[305]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombian_assistance">Colombian assistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Colombian assistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2015, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> sent a delegation of security experts to assist the Nigerian authorities and share expertise on security and counter terrorism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> In January 2016, a delegation led by <a href="/wiki/Tukur_Yusuf_Buratai" title="Tukur Yusuf Buratai">Lieutenant General Tukur Yusuf Buratai</a> also visited Colombia to exchange information in regards to the war against Boko Haram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boko_Haram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="Book icon" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="30" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/30px-Office-book.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/45px-Office-book.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Office-book.svg/60px-Office-book.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>
